				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-subjectpageheader">
</div><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Philosophical concept</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>, the <b>"hard problem" of consciousness</b> is to explain why and how humans (and other organisms) have <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phenomenal_consciousness" class="mw-redirect" title="Phenomenal consciousness">phenomenal consciousness</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Subjective_character_of_experience" title="Subjective character of experience">subjective experience</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Harnad&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harnad-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is contrasted with the "easy problems" of explaining why and how physical systems give a human being the ability to discriminate, to integrate information, and to perform behavioural functions such as watching, listening, speaking (including generating an utterance that appears to refer to personal behaviour or belief), and so forth.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The easy problems are amenable to functional explanation—that is, explanations that are mechanistic or behavioural—since each physical system can be explained purely by reference to the "structure and dynamics" that underpin the phenomenon.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cooney&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooney-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Proponents of the hard problem propose that it is categorically different from the easy problems since no mechanistic or behavioural explanation could explain the character of an <a href="/wiki/Experience" title="Experience">experience</a>, not even in principle. Even after all the relevant functional facts are explicated, they argue, there will still remain a further question: "why is the performance of these functions accompanied by experience?"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To bolster their case, proponents of the hard problem frequently turn to various philosophical <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiments" class="mw-redirect" title="Thought experiments">thought experiments</a>, involving <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_zombie" title="Philosophical zombie">philosophical zombies</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Inverted_spectrum" title="Inverted spectrum">inverted qualia</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_Argument" class="mw-redirect" title="Knowledge Argument">ineffability of colour experiences</a>, or the unknowability of foreign states of consciousness, such as the experience of <a href="/wiki/What_is_it_like_to_be_a_bat%3F" class="mw-redirect" title="What is it like to be a bat?">being a bat</a>.
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:David_Chalmers,_delivering_a_talk_at_De_La_Salle_University-Manila,_March_27,_2012.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/David_Chalmers%2C_delivering_a_talk_at_De_La_Salle_University-Manila%2C_March_27%2C_2012.jpg/250px-David_Chalmers%2C_delivering_a_talk_at_De_La_Salle_University-Manila%2C_March_27%2C_2012.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/David_Chalmers%2C_delivering_a_talk_at_De_La_Salle_University-Manila%2C_March_27%2C_2012.jpg/500px-David_Chalmers%2C_delivering_a_talk_at_De_La_Salle_University-Manila%2C_March_27%2C_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/David_Chalmers%2C_delivering_a_talk_at_De_La_Salle_University-Manila%2C_March_27%2C_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="333" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/David_Chalmers" title="David Chalmers">David Chalmers</a> on stage for an <a href="/wiki/Alan_Turing_Year" title="Alan Turing Year">Alan Turing Year</a> event at <a href="/wiki/De_La_Salle_University" title="De La Salle University">De La Salle University</a>, Manila, 27 March 2012</figcaption></figure>
<p>The terms "hard problem" and "easy problems" were coined by the <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/David_Chalmers" title="David Chalmers">David Chalmers</a> in a 1994 talk given at <a href="/wiki/The_Science_of_Consciousness" title="The Science of Consciousness">The Science of Consciousness</a> conference held in Tucson, Arizona.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The following year, the main talking points of Chalmers' talk were published in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Journal_of_Consciousness_Studies" class="mw-redirect" title="The Journal of Consciousness Studies">The Journal of Consciousness Studies</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The publication gained significant attention from consciousness researchers and became the subject of a special volume of the journal,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> which was later published into a book.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1996, Chalmers published <i><a href="/wiki/The_Conscious_Mind" title="The Conscious Mind">The Conscious Mind</a></i>, a book-length treatment of the hard problem, in which he elaborated on his core arguments and responded to <a href="/wiki/Counterarguments" class="mw-redirect" title="Counterarguments">counterarguments</a>. His use of the word <i>easy</i> is "tongue-in-cheek".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As the <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_psychologist" class="mw-redirect" title="Cognitive psychologist">cognitive psychologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Steven_Pinker" title="Steven Pinker">Steven Pinker</a> puts it, they are about as easy as going to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> or curing cancer. "That is, scientists more or less know what to look for, and with enough brainpower and funding, they would probably crack it in this century."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pinker-time&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinker-time-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The existence of the hard problem is disputed. It has been accepted by some <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophers of mind</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Levine_(philosopher)" title="Joseph Levine (philosopher)">Joseph Levine</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Colin_McGinn" title="Colin McGinn">Colin McGinn</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mcginn-2012&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcginn-2012-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ned_Block" title="Ned Block">Ned Block</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-harder-problem&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harder-problem-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_neuroscience" title="Cognitive neuroscience">cognitive neuroscientists</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Francisco_Varela" title="Francisco Varela">Francisco Varela</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Tononi" title="Giulio Tononi">Giulio Tononi</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-iit-koch-et-al&#95;14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iit-koch-et-al-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-tononi+koch&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tononi+koch-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Christof_Koch" title="Christof Koch">Christof Koch</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-iit-koch-et-al&#95;14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iit-koch-et-al-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-tononi+koch&#95;15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tononi+koch-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On the other hand, its existence is rejected by other philosophers of mind, such as <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dennett&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Massimo_Pigliucci" title="Massimo Pigliucci">Massimo Pigliucci</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pigliucci2013&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigliucci2013-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Metzinger" title="Thomas Metzinger">Thomas Metzinger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Churchland" title="Patricia Churchland">Patricia Churchland</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pat-churchland-1996&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pat-churchland-1996-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Keith_Frankish" title="Keith Frankish">Keith Frankish</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-frankish&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frankish-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and by cognitive neuroscientists such as <a href="/wiki/Stanislas_Dehaene" title="Stanislas Dehaene">Stanislas Dehaene</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dehaene&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dehaene-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Baars" title="Bernard Baars">Bernard Baars</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dc-gw&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dc-gw-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anil_Seth" title="Anil Seth">Anil Seth</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-seth-aeon&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seth-aeon-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Damasio" title="Antonio Damasio">Antonio Damasio</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Clinical neurologist and sceptic <a href="/wiki/Steven_Novella" title="Steven Novella">Steven Novella</a> has dismissed it as "the hard non-problem".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to a 2020 <a href="/wiki/PhilPapers" title="PhilPapers">PhilPapers</a> survey, a majority (62.4%) of the philosophers surveyed said they believed that the hard problem is a genuine problem, while 29.7% said that it does not exist.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-philpapers2020&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-philpapers2020-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There are a number of other potential <a href="/wiki/List_of_philosophical_problems" title="List of philosophical problems">philosophical problems</a> that are related to the Hard Problem. Ned Block believes that there exists a "Harder Problem of Consciousness", due to the possibility of different physical and functional neurological systems potentially having phenomenal overlap.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-harder-problem&#95;12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harder-problem-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another potential philosophical problem which is closely related to Benj Hellie's <a href="/wiki/Vertiginous_question" title="Vertiginous question">vertiginous question</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> dubbed "The Even Harder Problem of Consciousness", refers to why a given individual has their own particular <a href="/wiki/Personal_identity" title="Personal identity">personal identity</a>, as opposed to existing as someone else.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Overview">Overview</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Cognitive scientist <a href="/wiki/David_Chalmers" title="David Chalmers">David Chalmers</a> first formulated the hard problem in his paper "Facing up to the problem of consciousness" (1995)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and expanded upon it in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Conscious_Mind" title="The Conscious Mind">The Conscious Mind</a></i> (1996). His works provoked comment. Some, such as philosopher <a href="/wiki/David_Lewis_(philosopher)" title="David Lewis (philosopher)">David Lewis</a> and Steven Pinker, have praised Chalmers for his argumentative rigour and "impeccable clarity".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pinker later said, in 2018, "In the end I still think that the hard problem is a meaningful conceptual problem, but agree with Dennett that it is not a meaningful scientific problem. No one will ever get a grant to study whether you are a zombie or whether the same Captain Kirk walks on the deck of the Enterprise and the surface of Zakdorn. And I agree with several other philosophers that it may be futile to hope for a solution at all, precisely because it is a conceptual problem, or, more accurately, a problem with our concepts."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Churchland" title="Patricia Churchland">Patricia Churchland</a>, among others, believe that the hard problem is best seen as a collection of easy problems that will be solved through further analysis of the brain and behaviour.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Consciousness is an ambiguous term. It can be used to mean self consciousness, awareness, the state of being awake, and so on. Chalmers uses <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nagel" title="Thomas Nagel">Thomas Nagel</a>'s definition of consciousness: "<i>the feeling of what it is like to be something."</i> Consciousness, in this sense, is synonymous with <i>experience.</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bat&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bat-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Chalmers'_formulation"><span id="Chalmers.27_formulation"></span>Chalmers' formulation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Chalmers&#039; formulation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>. . .even when we have explained the performance of all the cognitive and behavioral functions in the vicinity of experience—perceptual discrimination, categorization, internal access, verbal report—there may still remain a further unanswered question: <i>Why is the performance of these functions accompanied by experience?</i></p></blockquote><div class="templatequotecite"><p style="display: inline; padding-left: 2.3em;">—&#8202;David Chalmers, Facing up to the problem of consciousness<span style="display:none" data-plural="1"></span></p></div><p>The problems of consciousness, Chalmers argues, are of two kinds: the <i>easy problems</i> and the <i>hard problem</i>.
</p><div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Easy_problems">Easy problems</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Easy problems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The easy problems are amenable to reductive enquiry. They are a <a href="/wiki/Logical_consequence" title="Logical consequence">logical consequence</a> of lower-level facts about the world, similar to how a clock's ability to tell time is a logical consequence of its clockwork and structure, or a hurricane being a logical consequence of the structures and functions of certain weather patterns. A clock, a hurricane, and the easy problems, are all the sum of their parts (as are most things).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The easy problems relevant to consciousness concern mechanistic analysis of the neural processes that accompany behaviour. Examples of these include how sensory systems work, how sensory data is processed in the brain, how that data influences behaviour or verbal reports, the neural basis of thought and emotion, and so on. They are problems that can be analysed through "structures and functions".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;28-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Hard_problem">Hard problem</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Hard problem"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The hard problem, in contrast, is the problem of <i>why</i> and <i>how</i> those processes are accompanied by experience.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It may further include the question of why these processes are accompanied by this or that particular experience, rather than some other kind of experience. In other words, the hard problem is the problem of explaining why certain mechanisms are accompanied by conscious experience.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;28-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, why should neural processing in the brain lead to the felt sensations of, say, feelings of hunger? And why should those neural firings lead to feelings of hunger rather than some other feeling (such as, for example, feelings of thirst)?
</p><p>Chalmers argues that it is conceivable that the relevant behaviours associated with hunger, or any other feeling, could occur even in the absence of that feeling. This suggests that experience is <a href="/wiki/Irreducibility" class="mw-redirect" title="Irreducibility">irreducible</a> to physical systems such as the brain. This is the topic of the next section.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="How_the_easy_and_hard_problems_are_related">How the easy and hard problems are related</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: How the easy and hard problems are related"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Chalmers believes that the hard problem is irreducible to the easy problems: solving the easy problems will not lead to a solution to the hard problem. This is because the easy problems pertain to the causal structure of the world while the hard problem pertains to consciousness, and facts about consciousness include facts that go beyond mere causal or structural description.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>
For example, suppose someone were to stub their foot and yelp. In this scenario, the easy problems are mechanistic explanations that involve the activity of the nervous system and brain and its relation to the environment (such as the propagation of nerve signals from the toe to the brain, the processing of that information and how it leads to yelping, and so on). The hard problem is the question of why these mechanisms are accompanied by <i>the feeling of pain</i>, or why these feelings of pain feel the particular way that they do. Chalmers argues that facts about the neural mechanisms of pain, and pain behaviours, do not lead to facts about conscious experience. Facts about conscious experience are, instead, <a href="/wiki/Further_facts" title="Further facts">further facts</a>, not derivable from facts about the brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;28-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p><figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Inverted_qualia_of_colour_strawberry.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Inverted_qualia_of_colour_strawberry.jpg/250px-Inverted_qualia_of_colour_strawberry.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="143" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Inverted_qualia_of_colour_strawberry.jpg/500px-Inverted_qualia_of_colour_strawberry.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1070" data-file-height="614" /></a><figcaption>The hard problem is often illustrated by appealing to the logical possibility of inverted visible spectra. If there is no logical contradiction in supposing that one's colour vision could be inverted, it follows that mechanistic explanations of visual processing do not determine facts about what it is like to see colours.</figcaption></figure><p> An explanation for all of the relevant physical facts about neural processing would leave unexplained facts about what it is like to feel pain. This is in part because functions and physical structures of any sort could conceivably exist in the absence of experience. Alternatively, they could exist alongside a different set of experiences. For example, it is <a href="/wiki/Logical_possibility" title="Logical possibility">logically possible</a> for a perfect replica of Chalmers to have no experience at all, or for it to have a different set of experiences (such as an inverted visible spectrum, so that the blue-yellow red-green axes of its visual field are flipped).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The same cannot be said about clocks, hurricanes, or other physical things. In those cases, a structural or functional description is a complete description. A perfect replica of a clock is a clock, a perfect replica of a hurricane is a hurricane, and so on. The difference is that physical things are nothing more than their physical constituents. For example, water is nothing more than H<sub>2</sub>O molecules, and understanding everything about H<sub>2</sub>O molecules is to understand everything there is to know about water. But consciousness is not like this. Knowing everything there is to know about the brain, or any physical system, is not to know everything there is to know about consciousness. Consciousness, then, must not be purely physical.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;28-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Implications_for_physicalism">Implications for physicalism</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Implications for physicalism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1320445320">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">Physicalism</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><span><video id="mwe_player_0" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Rotterdam%2C_South_Holland%2C_Netherlands_-flock-8.ogv/250px--Sturnus_vulgaris_-Rotterdam%2C_South_Holland%2C_Netherlands_-flock-8.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" data-mw-tmh="" class="mw-file-element" width="250" height="141" data-durationhint="75" data-mwtitle="Sturnus_vulgaris_-Rotterdam,_South_Holland,_Netherlands_-flock-8.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" resource="/wiki/File:Sturnus_vulgaris_-Rotterdam,_South_Holland,_Netherlands_-flock-8.ogv"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/93/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Rotterdam%2C_South_Holland%2C_Netherlands_-flock-8.ogv/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Rotterdam%2C_South_Holland%2C_Netherlands_-flock-8.ogv.360p.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp8, vorbis&quot;" data-transcodekey="360p.webm" data-width="640" data-height="360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/93/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Rotterdam%2C_South_Holland%2C_Netherlands_-flock-8.ogv" type="video/ogg; codecs=&quot;theora, vorbis&quot;" data-width="640" data-height="360" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/9/93/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Rotterdam%2C_South_Holland%2C_Netherlands_-flock-8.ogv/Sturnus_vulgaris_-Rotterdam%2C_South_Holland%2C_Netherlands_-flock-8.ogv.240p.vp9.webm" type="video/webm; codecs=&quot;vp9, opus&quot;" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" data-height="240" /></video></span><figcaption>A swarm of birds showing high order structure emerging from simpler physical constituents</figcaption></figure>
<p>Chalmers's idea contradicts <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalism</a>, sometimes labelled <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>. This is the view that everything that exists is a physical or material thing, so everything can be reduced to microphysical things. For example, the rings of Saturn are a physical thing because they are nothing more than a complex arrangement of a large number of subatomic particles interacting in a certain way. According to physicalism, everything, including consciousness, can be explained by appeal to its microphysical constituents. Chalmers's <i>hard problem</i> presents a <a href="/wiki/Counterexample" title="Counterexample">counterexample</a> to this view and to other phenomena like swarms of birds, since it suggests that consciousness, and analogously swarms of birds, cannot be reductively explained by appealing to their physical constituents. Thus, if the hard problem is a real problem then physicalism must be false, and if physicalism is true then the hard problem must not be a real problem.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Though Chalmers rejects physicalism, he is still a <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(philosophy)" title="Naturalism (philosophy)">naturalist</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;28-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Encyclopedic_content" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not"><span title="The material preceding this tag may lack sufficient importance. (April 2023)">importance?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Christian_List" title="Christian List">Christian List</a> argues that the existence of first-person perspectives and the inability for physicalism to answer Hellie's vertiginous question is evidence against physicalism, since first-personal facts cannot supervene on physical third-personal facts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> List also claims that there exists a "quadrilemma" for metaphysical theories of consciousness, and that for the metaphysical claims of first-person realism, non-solipsism, non-fragmentation, and one-world, at least one of these must be false.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> List has proposed a model he calls the "many-worlds theory of consciousness" in order to reconcile the subjective nature of consciousness without lapsing into solipsism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Historical_precedents">Historical precedents</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Historical precedents"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1314755338">.mw-parser-output .side-box{margin:4px 0;box-sizing:border-box;border:1px solid #aaa;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em;background-color:var(--background-color-interactive-subtle,#f8f9fa);color:inherit;display:flow-root}.mw-parser-output .infobox .side-box{font-size:100%}.mw-parser-output .side-box-abovebelow,.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{padding:0.25em 0.9em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-image{padding:2px 0 2px 0.9em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-imageright{padding:2px 0.9em 2px 0;text-align:center}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .side-box-flex{display:flex;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .side-box-text{flex:1;min-width:0}}@media(min-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .side-box{width:238px}.mw-parser-output .side-box-right{clear:right;float:right;margin-left:1em}.mw-parser-output .side-box-left{margin-right:1em}}</style><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1311551236">@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .sistersitebox{display:none!important}}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .sistersitebox img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .sistersitebox img[src*="Wiktionary-logo-en-v2.svg"]{filter:invert(1)brightness(55%)contrast(250%)hue-rotate(180deg)}}</style><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1126788409">.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul{line-height:inherit;list-style:none;margin:0;padding:0}.mw-parser-output .plainlist ol li,.mw-parser-output .plainlist ul li{margin-bottom:0}</style>
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikiquote-logo.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikiquote-logo.svg/40px-Wikiquote-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="34" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikiquote-logo.svg/60px-Wikiquote-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikiquote-logo.svg/120px-Wikiquote-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="355" /></a></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Wikiquote has quotations related to <i><b><a href="https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Special:Search/Hard_problem_of_consciousness" class="extiw" title="q:Special:Search/Hard problem of consciousness">Hard problem of consciousness</a></b></i>.</div></div>
</div>
<p>The hard problem of consciousness has scholarly antecedents considerably earlier than Chalmers. Chalmers himself notes that "a number of thinkers in the recent and distant past" have "recognised the particular difficulties of explaining consciousness."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moving&#95;forward&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moving_forward-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He states that all his original 1996 paper contributed to the discussion was "a catchy name, a minor reformulation of philosophically familiar points".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Moving&#95;forward&#95;37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moving_forward-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Among others, thinkers who have made arguments similar to Chalmers' formulation of the hard problem include <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-2020&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-2020-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Locke" title="John Locke">John Locke</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Aranyosi&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Aranyosi-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-2020&#95;38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-2020-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/John_Stuart_Mill" title="John Stuart Mill">John Stuart Mill</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">Thomas Henry Huxley</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-2020&#95;38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-2020-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Likewise, Asian philosophers like <a href="/wiki/Dharmakirti" title="Dharmakirti">Dharmakirti</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guifeng_Zongmi" title="Guifeng Zongmi">Guifeng Zongmi</a> discussed the problem of how consciousness arises from unconscious matter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-2020&#95;38-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-2020-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-arnold-2021&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arnold-2021-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Tattva Bodha, an eighth century text attributed to <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Adi Shankara</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Advaita_Vedanta" title="Advaita Vedanta">Advaita Vedanta</a> school of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, describes consciousness being <i>anubhati</i>, or self-revealing, illuminating all objects of knowledge without itself being a material object.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Related_concepts">Related concepts</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Related concepts"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_mind–body_problem"><span id="The_mind.E2.80.93body_problem"></span>The mind–body problem</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: The mind–body problem"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">Mind–body problem</a></div>
<p>The mind–body problem is the problem of how the mind and the body relate. The mind-body problem is more general than the hard problem of consciousness, since it is the problem of discovering how the mind and body relate in general, thereby implicating any theoretical framework that broaches the topic. The hard problem, in contrast, is often construed as a problem uniquely faced by physicalist or materialist theories of mind.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="&quot;What_Is_It_Like_to_Be_a_Bat?&quot;"><span id=".22What_Is_It_Like_to_Be_a_Bat.3F.22"></span>"What Is It Like to Be a Bat?"</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: &quot;What Is It Like to Be a Bat?&quot;"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/What_Is_It_Like_to_Be_a_Bat%3F" title="What Is It Like to Be a Bat?">What Is It Like to Be a Bat?</a></div>
<p>The philosopher Thomas Nagel posited in his 1974 paper "What Is It Like to Be a Bat?" that experiences are essentially subjective (accessible only to the individual undergoing them—i.e., felt only by the one feeling them), while physical states are essentially objective (accessible to multiple individuals). So he argued we have no idea what it could mean to claim that an essentially subjective state just <i>is</i> an essentially non-subjective state (i.e., that a felt state is nothing but a functional state). In other words, we have no idea of what reductivism amounts to.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bat&#95;32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bat-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He believes "every subjective phenomenon is essentially connected with a single point of view, and it seems inevitable that an objective, physical theory will abandon that point of view."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bat&#95;32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bat-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Explanatory_gap">Explanatory gap</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Explanatory gap"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Explanatory_gap" title="Explanatory gap">Explanatory gap</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Reductionism" title="Reductionism">Reductionism</a></div>
<p>In 1983, the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Levine_(philosopher)" title="Joseph Levine (philosopher)">Joseph Levine</a> proposed that there is an <i>explanatory gap</i> between our understanding of the physical world and our understanding of consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Levine1983&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levine1983-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Levine's disputes that conscious states are reducible to neuronal or brain states. He uses the example of pain (as an example of a conscious state) and its reduction to the firing of <a href="/wiki/Group_C_nerve_fiber" title="Group C nerve fiber">c-fibers</a> (a kind of nerve cell). The difficulty is as follows: even if consciousness is physical, it is not clear which physical states correspond to which conscious states. The bridges between the two levels of description will be <a href="/wiki/Contingency_(philosophy)" title="Contingency (philosophy)">contingent</a>, rather than <a href="/wiki/Necessity_and_sufficiency" title="Necessity and sufficiency">necessary</a>. This is significant because in most contexts, relating two scientific levels of descriptions (such as physics and chemistry) is done with the assurance of necessary connections between the two theories (for example, chemistry follows with necessity from physics).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Levine illustrates this with a thought experiment: Suppose that humanity were to encounter an alien species, and suppose it is known that the aliens do not have any c-fiber. Even if one knows this, it is not obvious that the aliens do not feel pain: that would remain an open question. This is because the fact that aliens do not have c-fibers does not entail that they do not feel pain (in other words, feelings of pain do not follow with logical necessity from the firing of c-fibers). Levine thinks such thought experiments demonstrate an explanatory gap between consciousness and the physical world: even if consciousness is reducible to physical things, consciousness cannot be explained in terms of physical things, because the link between physical things and consciousness is a contingent link.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Levine does not think that the explanatory gap means that consciousness is not physical; he is open to the idea that the explanatory gap is only an <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemological</a> problem for physicalism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In contrast, Chalmers thinks that the hard problem of consciousness does show that consciousness is not physical.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;28-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Philosophical_zombies">Philosophical zombies</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Philosophical zombies"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_zombie" title="Philosophical zombie">Philosophical zombie</a></div>
<p>Philosophical zombies are a thought experiment commonly used in discussions of the hard problem.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They are hypothetical beings physically identical to humans but that lack conscious experience.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Philosophers such as Chalmers, Joseph Levine, and Saul Kripke take zombies as impossible within the bounds of nature but possible within the bounds of logic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This would imply that facts about experience are not logically entailed by the "physical" facts. Therefore, consciousness is irreducible. In Chalmers' words, "after God (hypothetically) created the world, he had more work to do."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Daniel Dennett, a philosopher of mind, criticised the field's use of "the zombie hunch" which he deems an "embarrassment"<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that ought to "be dropped like a hot potato".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Knowledge_argument">Knowledge argument</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Knowledge argument"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Knowledge_argument" title="Knowledge argument">Knowledge argument</a></div>
<p>The knowledge argument, also known as <i>Mary's Room</i>, is another common thought experiment: A hypothetical neuroscientist named Mary has lived her whole life in a black-and-white room and has never seen colour before. She also happens to know everything there is to know about the brain and colour perception.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nida-Rümelin2019&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nida-Rümelin2019-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chalmers believes<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:3&#95;54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that when Mary sees the colour red for the first time, she gains new knowledge — the knowledge of "what red looks like" — which is distinct from, and irreducible to, her prior physical knowledge of the brain or visual system. A stronger form of the knowledge argument<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nida-Rümelin2019&#95;56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nida-Rümelin2019-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> claims not merely that Mary would lack subjective <i>knowledge</i> of "what red looks like," but that she would lack knowledge of an objective <i>fact</i> about the world: namely, "what red looks like," a non-physical fact that can be learned only through direct experience (qualia). Others, such as Thomas Nagel, take a "<a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalist</a>" position, disagree with the argument in its stronger and/or weaker forms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nida-Rümelin2019&#95;56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nida-Rümelin2019-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nagel" title="Thomas Nagel">Nagel</a> put forward a "speculative proposal" of devising a language that could "explain to a person blind from birth what it is like to see."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bat&#95;32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bat-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The knowledge argument implies that such a language could not exist.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Philosophical_responses">Philosophical responses</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Philosophical responses"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/David_Chalmers" title="David Chalmers">David Chalmers'</a> formulation of the hard problem of consciousness provoked considerable debate within <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>, as well as scientific research.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Dualism-vs-Monism.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dualism-vs-Monism.png/250px-Dualism-vs-Monism.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="137" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Dualism-vs-Monism.png/500px-Dualism-vs-Monism.png 1.5x" data-file-width="651" data-file-height="356" /></a><figcaption>A diagram showing the relationship between various views concerning the relationship between consciousness and the physical world</figcaption></figure>
<p>The hard problem is considered a problem primarily for <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalist</a> views of the mind (the view that the mind is a physical object or process), since physical explanations tend to be functional, or structural. Because of this, some physicalists have responded to the hard problem by seeking to show that it dissolves upon analysis. Other researchers accept the problem as real and seek to develop a theory of consciousness' place in the world that can solve it, by either modifying physicalism or abandoning it in favour of an alternative <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontology</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Panpsychism" title="Panpsychism">panpsychism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_dualism" title="Mind–body dualism">dualism</a>).
A third response has been to accept the hard problem as real but deny human cognitive faculties can solve it.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/PhilPapers" title="PhilPapers">PhilPapers</a> is an organisation that archives academic philosophy papers and periodically surveys professional philosophers about their views. It can be used to gauge professional attitudes towards the hard problem. As of the 2020 survey results, it seems that the majority of philosophers (62.42%) agree that the hard problem is real, with a substantial minority that disagrees (29.76%).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-philpapers2020&#95;25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-philpapers2020-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Attitudes towards physicalism also differ among professionals. In the 2009 PhilPapers survey, 56.5% of philosophers surveyed subscribed to physicalism and 27.1% of philosophers surveyed rejected physicalism. 16.4% fell into the "other" category.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the 2020 PhilPapers survey, 51.93% of philosophers surveyed indicated that they "accept or lean towards" physicalism and 32.08% indicated that they reject physicalism. 6.23% were "agnostic" or "undecided".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-philpapers2020&#95;25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-philpapers2020-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Different solutions have been proposed to the hard problem of consciousness. The sections below taxonomize the various responses to the hard problem. The shape of this taxonomy was first introduced by Chalmers in a 2003 literature review on the topic.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The labelling convention of this taxonomy has been incorporated into the technical vocabulary of analytic philosophy, being used by philosophers such as Adrian Boutel,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Raamy Majeed,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-majeed2016&#95;60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-majeed2016-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Janet Levin,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Levin&#95;2008&#95;402–425&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levin_2008_402–425-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pete Mandik &amp; Josh Weisberg,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Roberto Pereira,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Helen Yetter-Chappell.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Type-A_Materialism">Type-A Materialism</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Type-A Materialism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Reductive_materialism" class="mw-redirect" title="Reductive materialism">Reductive materialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/A_priori_physicalism" class="mw-redirect" title="A priori physicalism">A priori physicalism</a></div>
<p>Type-A materialism (also known as <i>reductive materialism</i> or <i>a priori physicalism</i>) is a view characterised by a commitment to <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalism</a> and a full rejection of the hard problem. By this view, the hard problem either does not exist or is just another easy problem, because every fact about the mind is a fact about the performance of various functions or behaviours. So, once all the relevant functions and behaviours have been accounted for, there will not be any facts left over in need of explanation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thinkers who subscribe to type-A materialism include <a href="/wiki/Paul_Churchland" title="Paul Churchland">Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Churchland" title="Patricia Churchland">Patricia Churchland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Keith_Frankish" title="Keith Frankish">Keith Frankish</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Metzinger" title="Thomas Metzinger">Thomas Metzinger</a>.
</p><p>Some type-A materialists believe in the reality of phenomenal consciousness but believe it is nothing extra in addition to certain functions or behaviours. This view is sometimes referred to as <i>strong reductionism</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other type-A materialists may reject the existence of phenomenal consciousness entirely. This view is referred to as <a href="/wiki/Eliminative_materialism" title="Eliminative materialism">eliminative materialism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Illusionism_(consciousness)" class="mw-redirect" title="Illusionism (consciousness)">illusionism</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-elim&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-elim-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-frankish-2016&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frankish-2016-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-dennett&#95;2016&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dennett_2016-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Strong_reductionism">Strong reductionism</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Strong reductionism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Many philosophers have disputed that there is a hard problem of consciousness distinct from what Chalmers calls the easy problems of consciousness. Some among them, who are sometimes termed <i>strong reductionists</i>, hold that <a href="/wiki/Phenomenal_consciousness" class="mw-redirect" title="Phenomenal consciousness">phenomenal consciousness</a> (i.e., conscious experience) does exist but that it can be fully understood as reducible to the brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Broadly, strong reductionists accept that conscious experience is real but argue it can be fully understood in functional terms as an emergent property of the material brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In contrast to weak reductionists (see above), strong reductionists reject ideas used to support the existence of a hard problem (that the same functional organization could exist without consciousness, or that a blind person who understood vision through a textbook would not know everything about sight) as simply mistaken intuitions.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A notable family of strong reductionist accounts are the <a href="/wiki/Higher-order_theories_of_consciousness" title="Higher-order theories of consciousness">higher-order theories of consciousness</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2005, the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Peter_Carruthers_(philosopher)" title="Peter Carruthers (philosopher)">Peter Carruthers</a> wrote about "recognitional concepts of experience", that is, "a capacity to recognize [a] type of experience when it occurs in one's own mental life," and suggested that such a capacity could explain phenomenal consciousness without positing qualia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Carruthers&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Carruthers-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On the higher-order view, since consciousness is a representation, and representation is fully functionally analysable, there is no hard problem of consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The philosophers Glenn Carruthers and Elizabeth Schier said in 2012 that the main arguments for the existence of a hard problem—<a href="/wiki/Philosophical_zombies" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophical zombies">philosophical zombies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mary%27s_room" class="mw-redirect" title="Mary&#39;s room">Mary's room</a>, and <a href="/wiki/What_Is_it_Like_to_Be_a_Bat%3F" class="mw-redirect" title="What Is it Like to Be a Bat?">Nagel's bats</a>—are only persuasive if one already assumes that "consciousness must be independent of the structure and function of mental states, i.e. that there is a hard problem." Hence, the arguments <a href="/wiki/Beg_the_question" class="mw-redirect" title="Beg the question">beg the question</a>. The authors suggest that "instead of letting our conclusions on the thought experiments guide our theories of consciousness, we should let our theories of consciousness guide our conclusions from the thought experiments."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-CarruthersSchier2012&#95;70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CarruthersSchier2012-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Massimo_Pigliucci" title="Massimo Pigliucci">Massimo Pigliucci</a> argued in 2013 that the hard problem is misguided, resulting from a "category mistake".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pigliucci2013&#95;17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigliucci2013-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He said: "Of course an explanation isn't the same as an experience, but that's because the two are completely independent categories, like colors and triangles. It is obvious that I cannot experience what it is like to be you, but I can potentially have a complete explanation of how and why it is possible to be you."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pigliucci2013&#95;17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pigliucci2013-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2017, the philosopher Marco Stango, in a paper on <a href="/wiki/John_Dewey" title="John Dewey">John Dewey</a>'s approach to the problem of consciousness (which preceded Chalmers' formulation of the hard problem by over half a century), noted that Dewey's approach would see the hard problem as the consequence of an unjustified assumption that feelings and functional behaviours are not the same physical process: "For the Deweyan philosopher, the 'hard problem' of consciousness is a 'conceptual fact' only in the sense that it is a <em>philosophical mistake</em>: the mistake of failing to see that the physical can be had as an episode of immediate sentiency."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Metzinger" title="Thomas Metzinger">Thomas Metzinger</a> likens the hard problem of consciousness to <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vitalism</a>, a formerly widespread view in biology which was not so much solved as abandoned.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Metzinger&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Metzinger-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Brian Jonathan Garrett has also argued that the hard problem suffers from flaws analogous to those of vitalism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Peter_Hacker" title="Peter Hacker">Peter Hacker</a> argues that the hard problem is misguided in that it asks how consciousness can emerge from matter, whereas in fact sentience emerges from the evolution of living organisms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hacker&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hacker-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He states: "The hard problem isn't a hard problem at all. The really hard problems are the problems the scientists are dealing with. [...] The philosophical problem, like all philosophical problems, is a confusion in the conceptual scheme."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hacker&#95;74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hacker-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hacker's critique extends beyond Chalmers and the hard problem, being directed against contemporary philosophy of mind and neuroscience more broadly. Along with the neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Max_Bennett_(scientist)" title="Max Bennett (scientist)">Max Bennett</a>, he has argued that most of contemporary neuroscience remains implicitly <a href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">dualistic</a> in its conceptualisations and is predicated on the <i><a href="/wiki/Mereology" title="Mereology">mereological</a> fallacy</i> of ascribing psychological concepts to the brain that can properly be ascribed only to the person as a whole.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-schaal-review&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-schaal-review-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hacker further states that "consciousness studies", as it exists today, is "literally a total waste of time" and that "the conception of consciousness which they have is incoherent".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hacker&#95;74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hacker-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Eliminative_materialism_/_Illusionism"><span id="Eliminative_materialism_.2F_Illusionism"></span>Eliminative materialism / Illusionism</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Eliminative materialism / Illusionism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Eliminative_materialism" title="Eliminative materialism">Eliminative materialism</a></div>
<p>Eliminative materialism or eliminativism is the view that many or all of the <a href="/wiki/Mental_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Mental states">mental states</a> used in <a href="/wiki/Folk_psychology" title="Folk psychology">folk psychology</a> (i.e., common-sense ways of discussing the mind) do not, upon scientific examination, correspond to real brain mechanisms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-elim&#95;65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-elim-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According the 2020 <a href="/wiki/PhilPapers" title="PhilPapers">PhilPapers</a> survey, 4.51% of philosophers surveyed subscribe to eliminativism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-philpapers2020&#95;25-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-philpapers2020-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Churchland" title="Patricia Churchland">Patricia Churchland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Churchland" title="Paul Churchland">Paul Churchland</a> have famously applied eliminative materialism to <a href="/wiki/Propositional_attitudes" class="mw-redirect" title="Propositional attitudes">propositional attitudes</a>, philosophers including <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georges_Rey" title="Georges Rey">Georges Rey</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Keith_Frankish" title="Keith Frankish">Keith Frankish</a> have applied it to <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a> or <a href="/wiki/Phenomenal_consciousness" class="mw-redirect" title="Phenomenal consciousness">phenomenal consciousness</a> (i.e., conscious experience).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-elim&#95;65-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-elim-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On their view, it is mistaken not only to believe there is a hard problem of consciousness, but to believe phenomenal consciousness exists at all.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-frankish&#95;19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frankish-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-dennett&#95;2016&#95;67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dennett_2016-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This stance has recently taken on the name of <i>illusionism</i>: the view that phenomenal consciousness is an illusion. The term was popularized by the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Keith_Frankish" title="Keith Frankish">Keith Frankish</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-frankish-2016&#95;66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frankish-2016-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Frankish argues that "illusionism" is preferable to "eliminativism" for labelling the view that phenomenal consciousness is an illusion. More substantively, Frankish argues that illusionism about phenomenal consciousness is preferable to realism about phenomenal consciousness. He states: "Theories of consciousness typically address the hard problem. They accept that phenomenal consciousness is real and aim to explain how it comes to exist. There is, however, another approach, which holds that phenomenal consciousness is an illusion and aims to explain why it seems to exist."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-frankish&#95;19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frankish-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Frankish concludes that illusionism "replaces the hard problem with the illusion problem—the problem of explaining how the illusion of phenomenality arises and why it is so powerful."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-frankish&#95;19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frankish-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> was another prominent figure associated with illusionism. After Frankish published a paper in the <a href="/wiki/Journal_of_Consciousness_Studies" title="Journal of Consciousness Studies">Journal of Consciousness Studies</a> titled <i>Illusionism as a Theory of Consciousness,</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-frankish-2016&#95;66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-frankish-2016-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dennett responded with his own paper humorously titled <i>Illusionism as the Obvious Default Theory of Consciousness.</i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-dennett&#95;2016&#95;67-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dennett_2016-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dennett had been arguing for the illusory status of consciousness since early on in his career. For example, in 1979 he published a paper titled <i>On the Absence of <a href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(philosophy)" title="Phenomenology (philosophy)">Phenomenology</a></i> (where he argues for the nonexistence of phenomenal consciousness).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Similar ideas have been explicated in his 1991 book <a href="/wiki/Consciousness_Explained" title="Consciousness Explained">Consciousness Explained</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dennett1991&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett1991-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dennett argues that the so-called "hard problem" will be solved in the process of solving what Chalmers terms the "easy problems".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dennett&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He compares consciousness to stage magic and its capability to create extraordinary illusions out of ordinary things.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dennett3&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett3-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To show how people might be commonly fooled into overstating the accuracy of their introspective abilities, he describes a phenomenon called <a href="/wiki/Change_blindness" title="Change blindness">change blindness</a>, a visual process that involves failure to detect scenery changes in a series of alternating images.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dennett2&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett2-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears.
