(January 2021)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> He accordingly argues that consciousness need not be what it seems to be based on introspection. To address the question of the hard problem, or how and why physical processes give rise to experience, Dennett states that the phenomenon of having experience is nothing more than the performance of functions or the production of behaviour, which can also be referred to as the easy problems of consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dennett&#95;16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Thus, Dennett argues that the hard problem of experience is included among—not separate from—the easy problems, and therefore they can only be explained together as a cohesive unit.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dennett3&#95;78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett3-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Eliminativists differ on the role they believe <a href="/wiki/Intuition" title="Intuition">intuitive</a> judgement plays in creating the apparent reality of consciousness. The philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jacy_Reese_Anthis" title="Jacy Reese Anthis">Jacy Reese Anthis</a> is of the position that this issue is born of an overreliance on intuition, calling philosophical discussions on the topic of consciousness a form of "intuition jousting".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-anthis&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anthis-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> But when the issue is tackled with "formal argumentation" and "precise semantics" then the hard problem will dissolve.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-anthis&#95;80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anthis-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The philosopher Elizabeth Irvine, in contrast, can be read as having the opposite view, since she argues that phenomenal properties (that is, properties of consciousness) do not exist in our <a href="/wiki/Folk_psychology" title="Folk psychology">common-sense view of the world</a>. She states that "the hard problem of consciousness may not be a genuine problem for non-philosophers (despite its overwhelming obviousness to philosophers)."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A complete illusionist theory of consciousness must include the description of a <a href="/wiki/Mechanism_(biology)" title="Mechanism (biology)">mechanism</a> by which the illusion of subjective experience is had and reported by people. Various philosophers and scientists have proposed possible theories.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-meta-problem&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meta-problem-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For example, in his book <i>Consciousness and the Social Brain</i> neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Graziano" title="Michael Graziano">Michael Graziano</a> advocates what he calls <a href="/wiki/Attention_schema_theory" title="Attention schema theory">attention schema theory</a>, in which our perception of being conscious is merely an error in perception, held by brains which evolved to hold erroneous and incomplete models of their own internal workings, just as they hold erroneous and incomplete models of their own bodies and of the external world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Consciousness&#95;as&#95;engineering&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consciousness_as_engineering-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Consciousness&#95;as&#95;engineering2&#95;84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consciousness_as_engineering2-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading5"><h5 id="Criticisms">Criticisms</h5><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Criticisms"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The main criticisms of eliminative materialism and illusionism hinge on the counterintuitive nature of the view. Arguments of this form are called <i>Moorean Arguments</i>. A Moorean argument seeks to undermine the conclusion of an argument by asserting that the <a href="/wiki/Negation" title="Negation">negation</a> of that conclusion is more certain than the <a href="/wiki/Premise" title="Premise">premises</a> of the argument.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The roots of the Moorean Argument against illusionism extend back to <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">Augustine of Hippo</a> who stated that he could not be deceived regarding his own existence, since the very act of being deceived secures the existence of a being there to be the recipient of that deception.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1314755338" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1311551236" /><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409" />
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikisource-logo.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/40px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png" decoding="async" width="38" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/60px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Wikisource-logo.svg/120px-Wikisource-logo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="410" data-file-height="430" /></a></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">English <a href="/wiki/Wikisource" title="Wikisource">Wikisource</a> has original text related to this article:
<div style="margin-left: 10px;"><b><a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/en:Discourse_on_the_Method/Part_4" class="extiw" title="s:en:Discourse on the Method/Part 4">Descartes' Discourse on the Method/Part 4</a></b></div></div></div>
</div>
<p>In the Early-Modern era, these arguments were repopularized by <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, who coined the now famous phrase <i>"Je pense, donc je suis"</i> ("I think, therefore I am").<sup id="cite&#95;ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Descartes argued that even if he was maximally deceived (because, for example, an evil demon was manipulating all his senses) he would still know with certainty that his mind exists, because the state of being deceived requires a mind as a prerequisite.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This same general argumentative structure is still in use today. For example, in 2002 David Chalmers published an explicitly Moorean argument against illusionism. The argument goes like this: The reality of consciousness is more certain than any theoretical commitments (to, for example, physicalism) that may be motivating the illusionist to deny the existence of consciousness. The reason for this is because we have direct "acquaintance" with consciousness, but we do not have direct acquaintance with anything else (including anything that could inform our beliefs in consciousness being an illusion). In other words: consciousness can be known directly, so the reality of consciousness is more certain than any philosophical or scientific theory that says otherwise.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers202-illusionism&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers202-illusionism-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chalmers concludes that "there is little doubt that something like the Moorean argument is the reason that most people reject illusionism and many find it crazy."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers2020-illusionism&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers2020-illusionism-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Eliminative materialism and illusionism have been the subject of criticism within the popular press. One highly cited example comes from the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Galen_Strawson" title="Galen Strawson">Galen Strawson</a> who wrote an article in the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Review_of_Books" class="mw-redirect" title="New York Review of Books">New York Review of Books</a> titled "The Consciousness Deniers". In it, Strawson describes illusionism as the "silliest claim ever made", next to which "every known religious belief is only a little less sensible than the belief that the grass is green."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Another notable example comes from <a href="/wiki/Christof_Koch" title="Christof Koch">Christof Koch</a> (a neuroscientist and one of the leading proponents of <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Information_Theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Integrated Information Theory">Integrated Information Theory</a>) in his popular science book <i>The Feeling of Life Itself</i>. In the early pages of the book, Koch describes eliminativism as the "metaphysical counterpart to Cotard's syndrome, a psychiatric condition in which patients deny being alive."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Koch takes the prevalence of eliminativism as evidence that "much of twentieth-century analytic philosophy has gone to the dogs".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Frankish has responded to such criticisms by asserting that "qualia realists" have to conceive of qualia as being either observational or theoretical in nature. If conceived of as observational, then realists cannot claim that illusionists are leaving anything out of their theories of consciousness, as such a claim would presuppose qualia as having certain theoretical components. If conceived of as theoretical, then illusionists are simply denying the theoretical components of qualia but not the mere fact that they exist, which is what they're attempting to explain in the first place.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Type-B_Materialism">Type-B Materialism</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Type-B Materialism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Phenomenal_concept_strategy" title="Phenomenal concept strategy">Phenomenal concept strategy</a> and <a href="/wiki/A_posteriori_physicalism" class="mw-redirect" title="A posteriori physicalism">A posteriori physicalism</a></div>
<p>Type-B Materialism, also known as <i>Weak Reductionism</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">A Posteriori</a> Physicalism</i>, is the view that the hard problem stems from human psychology, and is therefore not indicative of a genuine <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontological</a> gap between consciousness and the physical world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Like Type-A Materialists, Type-B Materialists are committed to <a href="/wiki/Physicalism" title="Physicalism">physicalism</a>. Unlike Type-A Materialists, however, Type-B Materialists <i>do</i> accept inconceivability arguments often cited in support of the hard problem, but with a key caveat: that inconceivability arguments give us insight only into how the human mind <i>tends to conceptualize</i> the relationship between mind and matter, but not into what the true nature of this relationship actually is.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> According to this view, there is a gap between two ways of knowing (introspection and neuroscience) that will not be resolved by understanding all the underlying neurobiology, but still believe that consciousness and neurobiology are one and the same in reality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While Type-B Materialists all agree that intuitions about the hard problem are psychological rather than ontological in origin, they differ as to whether our intuitions about the hard problem are innate or culturally conditioned. This has been dubbed the "hard-wired/soft-wired distinction."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-DCuniversal&#95;97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCuniversal-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In relation to Type-B Materialism, those who believe that our intuitions about the hard problem are innate (and therefore common to all humans) subscribe to the "hard-wired view".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DCuniversal&#95;97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCuniversal-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Those that believe our intuitions are culturally conditioned subscribe to the "soft-wired view". Unless otherwise specified, the term <i>Type-B Materialism</i> refers to the hard-wired view.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DCuniversal&#95;97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCuniversal-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Notable philosophers who subscribe to Type-B Materialism include <a href="/wiki/David_Papineau" title="David Papineau">David Papineau</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Levine_(philosopher)" title="Joseph Levine (philosopher)">Joseph Levine</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-J.&#95;Levine&#95;1999,&#95;pp&#95;3&#95;99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J._Levine_1999,_pp_3-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and Janet Levine.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Levin&#95;2008&#95;402–425&#95;61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Levin_2008_402–425-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_&quot;hard-wired_view&quot;"><span id="The_.22hard-wired_view.22"></span>The "hard-wired view"</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: The &quot;hard-wired view&quot;"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Joseph Levine (who formulated the notion of the <a href="/wiki/Explanatory_gap" title="Explanatory gap">explanatory gap</a>) states: "The explanatory gap argument doesn't demonstrate a gap in nature, but a gap in our understanding of nature."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-J.&#95;Levine&#95;1999,&#95;pp&#95;3&#95;99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-J._Levine_1999,_pp_3-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He nevertheless contends that full scientific understanding will not close the gap,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and that analogous gaps do not exist for other <a href="/wiki/Identity_(philosophy)" title="Identity (philosophy)">identities</a> in nature, such as that between water and H<sub>2</sub>O.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-rjg-iep&#95;100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rjg-iep-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The philosophers <a href="/wiki/Ned_Block" title="Ned Block">Ned Block</a> and <a href="/wiki/Robert_Stalnaker" title="Robert Stalnaker">Robert Stalnaker</a> agree that facts about what a conscious experience is like to the one experiencing it cannot be deduced from knowing all the facts about the underlying physiology, but by contrast argue that such gaps of knowledge <i>are</i> also present in many other cases in nature, such as the distinction between water and H<sub>2</sub>O.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-block-stalnaker&#95;101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-block-stalnaker-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-harder-problem&#95;12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harder-problem-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>To explain why these two ways of knowing (i.e. third-person scientific observation and first-person introspection) yield such different understandings of consciousness, weak reductionists often invoke the <i>phenomenal concepts strategy</i>, which argues the difference stems from our inaccurate <a href="/wiki/Phenomenal_consciousness" class="mw-redirect" title="Phenomenal consciousness">phenomenal</a> concepts (i.e., how we think about consciousness), not from the nature of consciousness itself.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-stoljar-2005&#95;102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stoljar-2005-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-pceg&#95;103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-pceg-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By this view, the hard problem of consciousness stems from a dualism of concepts, not from a dualism of properties or substances.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_&quot;soft-wired_view&quot;"><span id="The_.22soft-wired_view.22"></span>The "soft-wired view"</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: The &quot;soft-wired view&quot;"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Some consciousness researchers have argued that the hard problem is a cultural artifact, unique to contemporary Western Culture. This is similar to Type-B Materialism, but it makes the further claim that the psychological facts that cause us to intuit the hard problem are not innate, but culturally conditioned. Notable researchers who hold this view include <a href="/wiki/Anna_Wierzbicka" title="Anna Wierzbicka">Anna Wierzbicka</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AW2019&#95;104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW2019-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hakwan Lau and Matthias Michel.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Wierzbicka (who is a linguist) argues that the vocabulary used by consciousness researchers (including words like <i>experience</i> and <i>consciousness</i>) are not universally translatable, and are "<a href="/wiki/Parochialism" title="Parochialism">parochially</a> English."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AW2019&#95;104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW2019-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Weirzbicka calls David Chalmers out by name for using these words, arguing that if philosophers "were to use panhuman concepts expressed in crosstranslatable words" (such as <i>know</i>, <i>think</i>, or <i>feel</i>) then the hard problem would dissolve.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AW2019&#95;104-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AW2019-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> David Chalmers has responded to these criticisms by saying that he will not "apologise for using technical terms in an academic article . . . they play a key role in efficient communication in every discipline, including Wierzbicka's".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DCuniversal&#95;97-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DCuniversal-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Type-C_Materialism">Type-C Materialism</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Type-C Materialism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Type-C materialists acknowledge a distinction between knowledge and experience<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ChalmersGreen-aia&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChalmersGreen-aia-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> without asserting a more complete explanation for the experiential phenomenon. One taking this view would admit that there is an <a href="/wiki/Explanatory_gap" title="Explanatory gap">explanatory gap</a> for which no answer to date may be satisfactory, but trust that inevitably the gap will be closed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is described by analogy to progression in other areas of science, such as <a href="/wiki/Mass-energy_equivalence" class="mw-redirect" title="Mass-energy equivalence">mass-energy equivalence</a> which would have been unfathomable in ancient times,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Abiogenesis" title="Abiogenesis">abiogenesis</a> which was once considered paradoxical from an evolutionary framework,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ChalmersGreen-aia&#95;106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChalmersGreen-aia-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or a suspected future <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_everything" title="Theory of everything">theory of everything</a> combining relativity and quantum mechanics. Similarly, type-C materialism posits that the problem of consciousness is a consequence of our ignorance<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dennett1991&#95;77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dennett1991-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but just as resolvable as any other question in <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a>.
</p><p>Because the explanatory question of consciousness is evaded, type-C materialism does not presuppose<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RomainBrette&#95;109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RomainBrette-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the descriptive question, for instance that there is any self-consciousness, wakefulness, or even sentience<sup id="cite&#95;ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in a rock. Principally, the basis for the argument arises from the apparently high correlation of consciousness with living brain tissue,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> thereby rejecting <a href="/wiki/Panpsychism" title="Panpsychism">panpsychism</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-RomainBrette&#95;109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RomainBrette-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> without explicitly formulating physical causation. More specifically this position denies the existence of <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_zombies" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophical zombies">philosophical zombies</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-CarruthersSchier2012&#95;70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CarruthersSchier2012-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> for which there is an absence of data and no proposed method of testing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Whether via the inconceivability or actual nonexistence of zombies, a contradiction is exposed nullifying the premise of the consciousness problem's "hardness".
</p><p>Type-C materialism is compatible with several cases and could collapse into one of these other metaphysical views<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> depending on scientific discovery and its interpretation. With evidence of <a href="/wiki/Emergence" title="Emergence">emergence</a>, it resolves to strong reductionism under type A. With a different, possibly cultural paradigm for understanding consciousness, it resolves to type-B materialism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;33-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If consciousness is explained by the <a href="/wiki/Quantum_mind" title="Quantum mind">quantum mind</a>, then it resolves to property dualism under type D.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Quantum&#95;Approaches&#95;to&#95;Consciousness&#95;114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Quantum_Approaches_to_Consciousness-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With characterisation of intrinsic properties in physics extending beyond structure and dynamics, it could resolve to type-F monism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Richard Brown has defended an unorthodox form of type-C materialism which states that the hard problem cannot be decided <i>a priori</i> and the two major positions (physicalism and dualism) can only be vindicated empirically, i.e. through scientific advances. His version of type-C materialism is unorthodox because he claims that it does not collapse into the other positions. He uses "reverse zombie" and "reverse knowledge" thought experiments (anti-dualist versions of the standard anti-physicalist arguments) to show that <i>a priori</i> arguments beg the question and are only useful for revealing one's own intuitions, whether physicalist or dualist. The only reason why such thought experiments, both anti-physicalist and anti-dualist, seem intuitive is because they are <i>prima facie</i> conceivable but not ideally conceivable, where ideal conceivability involves knowledge of the completed science and thus the ability to deduce <i>a priori</i> the discovered identities, in the same way that "water is H₂O" was discovered empirically but the identity is deducible <i>a priori</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Brown2010&#95;115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2010-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Type-D_and_Type-E_Dualism">Type-D and Type-E Dualism</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Type-D and Type-E Dualism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">Dualism (philosophy of mind)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Interactionism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Interactionism (philosophy of mind)">Interactionism (philosophy of mind)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Epiphenomenalism" title="Epiphenomenalism">Epiphenomenalism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Dualism_(philosophy_of_mind)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dualism (philosophy of mind)">Dualism</a> views consciousness as either a non-physical <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substance</a> separate from the brain or a non-physical <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">property</a> of the physical brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-iep-dualism&#95;116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iep-dualism-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dualism is the view that the mind is irreducible to the physical body.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-iep-dualism&#95;116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iep-dualism-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There are multiple dualist accounts of the causal relationship between the mental and the physical, of which interactionism and epiphenomenalism are the most common today. Interactionism posits that the mental and physical causally impact one another, and is associated with the thought of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a> (1596–1650).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Epiphenomenalism holds the mental is causally dependent on the physical, but does not in turn causally impact it.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In contemporary philosophy, interactionism has been defended by philosophers including <a href="/wiki/Martine_Nida-R%C3%BCmelin" title="Martine Nida-Rümelin">Martine Nida-Rümelin</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mnr-dualism&#95;117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mnr-dualism-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while epiphenomenalism has been defended by philosophers including <a href="/wiki/Frank_Cameron_Jackson" title="Frank Cameron Jackson">Frank Jackson</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-jackson-1&#95;118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jackson-1-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-jackson-2&#95;119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jackson-2-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (although Jackson later changed his stance to physicalism).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jackson-3&#95;120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jackson-3-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chalmers has also defended versions of both positions as plausible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Traditional dualists such as Descartes believed the mental and the physical to be two separate <a href="/wiki/Substance_theory" title="Substance theory">substances</a>, or fundamental types of entities (hence "<a href="/wiki/Substance_dualism" class="mw-redirect" title="Substance dualism">substance dualism</a>"); some more recent dualists, however, accept only one substance, the physical, but state it has both mental and physical <a href="/wiki/Property_(philosophy)" title="Property (philosophy)">properties</a> (hence "<a href="/wiki/Property_dualism" title="Property dualism">property dualism</a>").<sup id="cite&#95;ref-iep-dualism&#95;116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iep-dualism-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Type-F_Monism">Type-F Monism</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Type-F Monism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Panpsychism" title="Panpsychism">Panpsychism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutral_monism" title="Neutral monism">Neutral monism</a></div>
<p>Meanwhile, <a href="/wiki/Panpsychism" title="Panpsychism">panpsychism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neutral_monism" title="Neutral monism">neutral monism</a>, broadly speaking, view consciousness as intrinsic to matter.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In its most basic form, panpsychism holds that all physical entities have minds (though its proponents take more qualified positions),<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-panpsych&#95;121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-panpsych-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while neutral monism, in at least some variations, holds that entities are composed of a substance with mental and physical aspects—and is thus sometimes described as a type of panpsychism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-nm&#95;122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-nm-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Forms of panpsychism and neutral monism were defended in the early twentieth century by the psychologist <a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-panpsych&#95;124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-panpsych-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>note 2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-panpsych&#95;124-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-panpsych-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the physicist <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Eddington" title="Arthur Eddington">Arthur Eddington</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pcp-2016&#95;126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pcp-2016-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-iep-panpsych&#95;127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iep-panpsych-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_Russell" title="Bertrand Russell">Bertrand Russell</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-panpsych&#95;121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-panpsych-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-nm&#95;122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-nm-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and interest in these views has been revived in recent decades by philosophers including <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nagel" title="Thomas Nagel">Thomas Nagel</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-panpsych&#95;124-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-panpsych-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Galen_Strawson" title="Galen Strawson">Galen Strawson</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-panpsych&#95;124-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-panpsych-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-strawson&#95;128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strawson-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philip_Goff_(philosopher)" title="Philip Goff (philosopher)">Philip Goff</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-sep-panpsych&#95;124-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sep-panpsych-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and David Chalmers.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-panpsych&#95;121-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-panpsych-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chalmers describes his overall view as "naturalistic dualism",<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but he says panpsychism is in a sense a form of physicalism,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers-caipin&#95;58-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-caipin-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as does Strawson.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-strawson&#95;128-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-strawson-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Proponents of panpsychism argue it solves the hard problem of consciousness parsimoniously by making consciousness a fundamental feature of reality.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pn-goff&#95;129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pn-goff-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Idealism_and_cosmopsychism">Idealism and cosmopsychism</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Idealism and cosmopsychism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">Idealism</a></div>
<p>A traditional solution to the hard problem is <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a>, according to which consciousness is fundamental and not simply an emergent property of matter. It is claimed that this avoids the hard problem entirely.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Objective_idealism" title="Objective idealism">Objective idealism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosmopsychism" class="mw-redirect" title="Cosmopsychism">cosmopsychism</a> consider mind or consciousness to be the fundamental substance of the universe. Proponents claim that this approach is immune to both the hard problem of consciousness and the <a href="/wiki/Combination_problem" class="mw-redirect" title="Combination problem">combination problem</a> that affects panpsychism.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-shani-keppler-2018&#95;131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shani-keppler-2018-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-shani-2015&#95;132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shani-2015-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-albahari-2019&#95;133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-albahari-2019-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>From an idealist perspective, matter is a representation or image of mental processes. Supporters suggest that this avoids the problems associated with the materialist view of mind as an emergent property of a physical brain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-kastrup-2018&#95;134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kastrup-2018-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Critics argue that this then leads to a decombination problem: how is it possible to split a single, universal conscious experience into multiple, distinct conscious experiences? In response, Bernardo Kastrup claims that nature hints at a mechanism for this in the condition <a href="/wiki/Dissociative_identity_disorder" title="Dissociative identity disorder">dissociative identity disorder</a> (previously known as Multiple Personality Disorder).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Kastrup proposes dissociation as an example from nature showing that multiple minds with their own individual subjective experience could develop within a single universal mind.
</p><p>Cognitive psychologist <a href="/wiki/Donald_D._Hoffman" title="Donald D. Hoffman">Donald D. Hoffman</a> uses a mathematical model based around conscious agents, within a fundamentally conscious universe, to support <a href="/wiki/Panpsychism#Conscious_realism" title="Panpsychism">conscious realism</a> as a description of nature—one that falls within the objective idealism approaches to the hard problem: "The objective world, i.e., the world whose existence does not depend on the perceptions of a particular conscious agent, consists entirely of conscious agents."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hoffman-2008&#95;136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hoffman-2008-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>David Chalmers calls this form of idealism one of "the handful of promising approaches to the mind–body problem."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-idealism&#95;137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-idealism-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="New_mysterianism">New mysterianism</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: New mysterianism"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_mysterianism" title="New mysterianism">New mysterianism</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/New_mysterianism" title="New mysterianism">New mysterianism</a>, most significantly associated with the philosopher <a href="/wiki/Colin_McGinn" title="Colin McGinn">Colin McGinn</a>, proposes that the human mind, in its current form, will not be able to explain consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mcginn-1989&#95;138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcginn-1989-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-mcginn-2012&#95;11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcginn-2012-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> McGinn draws on <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a>'s distinction between problems, which are in principle solvable, and mysteries, which human cognitive faculties are unequipped to ever understand, and places the <a href="/wiki/Mind%E2%80%93body_problem" title="Mind–body problem">mind–body problem</a> in the latter category.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mcginn-1989&#95;138-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcginn-1989-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> His position is that a <a href="/wiki/Metaphysical_naturalism" title="Metaphysical naturalism">naturalistic</a> explanation does exist but that the human mind is <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_closure_(philosophy)" title="Cognitive closure (philosophy)">cognitively closed</a> to it due to its limited range of intellectual abilities.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mcginn-1989&#95;138-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcginn-1989-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He cites <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Fodor" title="Jerry Fodor">Jerry Fodor</a>'s concept of the <a href="/wiki/Modularity_of_mind" title="Modularity of mind">modularity of mind</a> in support of cognitive closure.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mcginn-1989&#95;138-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcginn-1989-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While in McGinn's strong form, new mysterianism states that the relationship between consciousness and the material world can <i>never</i> be understood by the human mind, there are also weaker forms that argue it cannot be understood within existing paradigms but that advances in science or philosophy may open the way to other solutions (see above).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The ideas of <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Nagel" title="Thomas Nagel">Thomas Nagel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Levine_(philosopher)" title="Joseph Levine (philosopher)">Joseph Levine</a> fall into the second category.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jw-iep&#95;49-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jw-iep-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Steven Pinker has also endorsed this weaker version of the view, summarising it as follows:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pinker-time&#95;9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinker-time-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>And then there is the theory put forward by philosopher Colin McGinn that our vertigo when pondering the Hard Problem is itself a quirk of our brains. The brain is a product of evolution, and just as animal brains have their limitations, we have ours. Our brains can't hold a hundred numbers in memory, can't visualize seven-dimensional space and perhaps can't intuitively grasp why neural information processing observed from the outside should give rise to subjective experience on the inside. This is where I place my bet, though I admit that the theory could be demolished when an unborn genius—a Darwin or Einstein of consciousness—comes up with a flabbergasting new idea that suddenly makes it all clear to us.</p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Commentary_on_the_problem's_explanatory_targets"><span id="Commentary_on_the_problem.27s_explanatory_targets"></span>Commentary on the problem's explanatory targets</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Commentary on the problem&#039;s explanatory targets"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Philosopher Raamy Majeed argued in 2016 that the hard problem is associated with two "explanatory targets":<sup id="cite&#95;ref-majeed2016&#95;60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-majeed2016-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>[PQ] Physical processing gives rise to experiences with a phenomenal character.</li>
<li>[Q] Our phenomenal qualities are thus-and-so.</li></ol>
<p>The first fact concerns the relationship between the physical and the phenomenal (i.e., how and why are some physical states <a href="/wiki/Feeling" title="Feeling">felt</a> states), whereas the second concerns the very nature of the phenomenal itself (i.e., what does the felt state <a href="/wiki/What_Is_it_Like_to_Be_a_Bat%3F" class="mw-redirect" title="What Is it Like to Be a Bat?">feel like</a>?).
</p><p>Wolfgang Fasching argues that the hard problem is not about qualia, but about the what-it-is-like-ness of experience in Nagel's sense—about the givenness of phenomenal contents:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Today there is a strong tendency to simply <i>equate</i> consciousness with the qualia. Yet there is clearly something not quite right about this. The "itchiness of itches" and the "hurtfulness of pain" are qualities we are conscious <i>of</i>. So philosophy of mind tends to treat consciousness as if it consisted simply of the contents of consciousness (the phenomenal qualities), while it really is precisely <i>consciousness</i> of contents, the very givenness of whatever is subjectively given. And therefore the problem of consciousness does not pertain so much to some alleged "mysterious, nonpublic objects", i.e. objects that seem to be only "visible" to the respective subject, but rather to the nature of "seeing" itself (and in today's philosophy of mind astonishingly little is said about the latter).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Relationship_to_scientific_frameworks">Relationship to scientific frameworks</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Relationship to scientific frameworks"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Most neuroscientists and cognitive scientists believe that Chalmers' alleged "hard problem" will be solved, or be shown to not be a real problem, in the course of the solution of the so-called "easy problems", although a significant minority disagrees.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pinker-time&#95;9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pinker-time-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-dennett-edge&#95;140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dennett-edge-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Questionable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="These are not surveys. (May 2022)">better&#160;source&#160;needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Neural_correlates_of_consciousness">Neural correlates of consciousness</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Neural correlates of consciousness"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Neural_correlates_of_consciousness" title="Neural correlates of consciousness">Neural correlates of consciousness</a></div>
<p>Since 1990, researchers including the molecular biologist <a href="/wiki/Francis_Crick" title="Francis Crick">Francis Crick</a> and the neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Christof_Koch" title="Christof Koch">Christof Koch</a> have made significant progress toward identifying which neurobiological events occur concurrently to the experience of subjective consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-KochEtAl&#95;141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KochEtAl-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These postulated events are referred to as <i>neural correlates of consciousness</i> or NCCs. However, this research arguably addresses the question of <i>which</i> neurobiological mechanisms are linked to consciousness but not the question of <i>why</i> they should give rise to consciousness at all, the latter being the hard problem of consciousness as Chalmers formulated it. In "On the Search for the Neural Correlate of Consciousness", Chalmers said he is confident that, granting the principle that something such as what he terms "global availability" can be used as an indicator of consciousness, the neural correlates will be discovered "in a century or two".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-ncc&#95;142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-ncc-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, he stated regarding their relationship to the hard problem of consciousness:
</p>
<blockquote><p>One can always ask why these processes of availability should give rise to consciousness in the first place. As yet we cannot explain why they do so, and it may well be that full details about the processes of availability will still fail to answer this question. Certainly, nothing in the standard methodology I have outlined answers the question; that methodology assumes a relation between availability and consciousness, and therefore does nothing to explain it. [...] So the hard problem remains. But who knows: Somewhere along the line we may be led to the relevant insights that show why the link is there, and the hard problem may then be solved.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-chalmers-ncc&#95;142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-chalmers-ncc-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>The neuroscientist and Nobel laureate <a href="/wiki/Eric_Kandel" title="Eric Kandel">Eric Kandel</a> wrote that locating the NCCs would not solve the hard problem, but rather one of the so-called easy problems to which the hard problem is contrasted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kandel&#95;143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Kandel went on to note Crick and Koch's suggestion that once the <a href="/wiki/Binding_problem" title="Binding problem">binding problem</a>—understanding what accounts for the unity of experience—is solved, it will be possible to solve the hard problem empirically.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kandel&#95;143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Anil_Seth" title="Anil Seth">Anil Seth</a> argued that emphasis on the so-called hard problem is a distraction from what he calls the "real problem": understanding the neurobiology underlying consciousness, namely the neural correlates of various conscious processes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-seth-aeon&#95;22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-seth-aeon-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This more modest goal is the focus of most scientists working on consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kandel&#95;143-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kandel-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Psychologist <a href="/wiki/Susan_Blackmore" title="Susan Blackmore">Susan Blackmore</a> believes, by contrast, that the search for the neural correlates of consciousness is futile and itself predicated on an erroneous belief in the hard problem of consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-blackmore&#95;144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-blackmore-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Computational_cognition">Computational cognition</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Computational cognition"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Computational_theory_of_mind" title="Computational theory of mind">Computational theory of mind</a></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Functionalism_(philosophy_of_mind)" title="Functionalism (philosophy of mind)">functionalist</a> view in <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_science" title="Cognitive science">cognitive science</a> holds that the mind is an information processing system, and that cognition and consciousness together are a form of <a href="/wiki/Computation" title="Computation">computation</a>. Cognition, distinct from consciousness, is explained by <a href="/wiki/Neural_computation" title="Neural computation">neural computation</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Computational_cognition" title="Computational cognition">computational theory of cognition</a>. The computational theory of mind asserts that not only cognition, but also phenomenal consciousness or <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a>, are computational. While the computation system is realised by neurons rather than electronics, in theory it would be possible for artificial intelligence to be conscious.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Integrated_information_theory">Integrated information theory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Integrated information theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Integrated_information_theory" title="Integrated information theory">Integrated information theory</a></div>
<p>Integrated information theory (IIT), developed by the neuroscientist and psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/Giulio_Tononi" title="Giulio Tononi">Giulio Tononi</a> in 2004 and more recently also advocated by Koch, is one of the most discussed models of consciousness in neuroscience and elsewhere.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-comp-iit&#95;145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-comp-iit-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-critique-iit&#95;146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critique-iit-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The theory proposes an <a href="/wiki/Identity_(philosophy)" title="Identity (philosophy)">identity</a> between consciousness and integrated information, with the latter item (denoted as Φ) defined mathematically and thus in principle measurable.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-critique-iit&#95;146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critique-iit-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-morch-iit&#95;147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-morch-iit-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The hard problem of consciousness, write Tononi and Koch, may indeed be intractable when working from matter to consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tononi+koch&#95;15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tononi+koch-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, because IIT inverts this relationship and works from phenomenological axioms to matter, they say it could be able to solve the hard problem.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-tononi+koch&#95;15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tononi+koch-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In this vein, proponents have said the theory goes beyond identifying human neural correlates and can be extrapolated to all physical systems. Tononi wrote (along with two colleagues):
</p>
<blockquote><p>While identifying the "neural correlates of consciousness" is undoubtedly important, it is hard to see how it could ever lead to a satisfactory explanation of what consciousness is and how it comes about. As will be illustrated below, IIT offers a way to analyse systems of mechanisms to determine if they are properly structured to give rise to consciousness, how much of it, and of which kind.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-iit-3.0&#95;148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-iit-3.0-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>As part of a broader critique of IIT, Michael Cerullo suggested that the theory's proposed explanation is in fact for what he dubs (following <a href="/wiki/Scott_Aaronson" title="Scott Aaronson">Scott Aaronson</a>) the "Pretty Hard Problem" of methodically inferring which physical systems are conscious—but would not solve Chalmers' hard problem.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-critique-iit&#95;146-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critique-iit-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> "Even if IIT is correct," he argues, "it does not explain why integrated information generates (or is) consciousness."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-critique-iit&#95;146-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-critique-iit-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Chalmers agrees that IIT, if correct, would solve the "Pretty Hard Problem" rather than the hard problem.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-mindt-2017&#95;149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mindt-2017-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Global_workspace_theory">Global workspace theory</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Global workspace theory"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Global_workspace_theory" title="Global workspace theory">Global workspace theory</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Global_workspace_theory" title="Global workspace theory">Global workspace theory</a> (GWT) is a <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_architecture" title="Cognitive architecture">cognitive architecture</a> and theory of consciousness proposed by the cognitive psychologist <a href="/wiki/Bernard_Baars" title="Bernard Baars">Bernard Baars</a> in 1988.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-baars-2005&#95;150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baars-2005-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Baars explains the theory with the metaphor of a theatre, with conscious processes represented by an illuminated stage.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-baars-2005&#95;150-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baars-2005-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This theatre integrates inputs from a variety of unconscious and otherwise autonomous networks in the brain and then broadcasts them to unconscious networks (represented in the metaphor by a broad, unlit "audience").<sup id="cite&#95;ref-baars-2005&#95;150-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-baars-2005-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The theory has since been expanded upon by other scientists including cognitive neuroscientist <a href="/wiki/Stanislas_Dehaene" title="Stanislas Dehaene">Stanislas Dehaene</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dehaene-naccache&#95;151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dehaene-naccache-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In his original paper outlining the hard problem of consciousness, Chalmers discussed GWT as a theory that only targets one of the "easy problems" of consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In particular, he said GWT provided a promising account of how information in the brain could become globally accessible, but argued that "now the question arises in a different form: why should global accessibility give rise to conscious experience? As always, this bridging question is unanswered."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chalmers&#95;1-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chalmers-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> J. W. Dalton similarly criticised GWT on the grounds that it provides, at best, an account of the cognitive <i>function</i> of consciousness, and fails to explain its experiential aspect.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dalton-1997&#95;152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dalton-1997-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By contrast, A. C. Elitzur argued: "While [GWT] does not address the 'hard problem', namely, the very nature of consciousness, it constrains any theory that attempts to do so and provides important insights into the relation between consciousness and cognition."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-elitzur-1997&#95;153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elitzur-1997-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For his part, Baars writes (along with two colleagues) that there is no hard problem of explaining <a href="/wiki/Qualia" title="Qualia">qualia</a> over and above the problem of explaining causal functions, because qualia are entailed by neural activity and themselves causal.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-dc-gw&#95;21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dc-gw-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dehaene, in his 2014 book <i><a href="/wiki/Consciousness_and_the_Brain" title="Consciousness and the Brain">Consciousness and the Brain</a></i>, rejected the concept of qualia and argued that Chalmers' "easy problems" of consciousness are actually the hard problems.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dehaene&#95;20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dehaene-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> He further stated that the "hard problem" is based only upon ill-defined intuitions that are continually shifting as understanding evolves:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dehaene&#95;20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dehaene-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Once our intuitions are educated by cognitive neuroscience and computer simulations, Chalmers' hard problem will evaporate. The hypothetical concept of qualia, pure mental experience, detached from any information-processing role, will be viewed as a peculiar idea of the prescientific era, much like <a href="/wiki/Vitalism" title="Vitalism">vitalism</a>... [Just as science dispatched vitalism] the science of consciousness will keep eating away at the hard problem of consciousness until it vanishes.</p></blockquote>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Meta-problem">Meta-problem</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Meta-problem"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 2018, Chalmers highlighted what he calls the "<b>meta-problem of consciousness</b>", another problem related to the hard problem of consciousness:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-meta-problem&#95;82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meta-problem-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>The meta-problem of consciousness is (to a first approximation) the problem of explaining why we think that there is a [hard] problem of consciousness.</p></blockquote>
<p>In his "second approximation", he says it is the problem of explaining the behaviour of "<a href="/wiki/Consciousness#Types_of_consciousness" title="Consciousness">phenomenal</a> reports", and the behaviour of expressing a belief that there is a hard problem of consciousness.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-meta-problem&#95;82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meta-problem-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Explaining its significance, he says:<sup id="cite&#95;ref-meta-problem&#95;82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meta-problem-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>Although the meta-problem is strictly speaking an easy problem, it is deeply connected to the hard problem. We can reasonably hope that a solution to the meta-problem will shed significant light on the hard problem. A particularly strong line holds that a solution to the meta-problem will solve or dissolve the hard problem. A weaker line holds that it will not remove the hard problem, but it will constrain the form of a solution.</p></blockquote><p>In other words, the 'strong line' holds that the solution to the meta-problem would provide an explanation of our beliefs about consciousness that is independent of consciousness. That might debunk our beliefs about consciousness, in the same way that (Chalmers suggests) explaining beliefs about god in evolutionary terms may provide arguments against theism itself.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: In popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tom_Stoppard" title="Tom Stoppard">Tom Stoppard</a>'s play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hard_Problem" title="The Hard Problem">The Hard Problem</a></i>, first produced in 2015, is named after the hard problem of consciousness, which Stoppard defines as having "subjective First Person experiences".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Notes">Notes</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Hard_problem_of_consciousness&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1327269900">.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}body.skin-vector-2022 .mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:27em}body.skin-vector-2022 .mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:22.5em}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=upper-alpha]{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=upper-roman]{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=lower-alpha]{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=lower-greek]{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .references[data-mw-group=lower-roman]{list-style-type:lower-roman}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-upper-alpha .references{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-upper-roman .references{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-lower-alpha .references{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-lower-greek .references{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output div.reflist-liststyle-lower-roman .references{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div>
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references" data-mw-group="note">
<li id="cite&#95;note-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-86">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"But, without any delusive representations of images or phantasms, I am most certain that I am, and that I know and delight in this. In respect to these truths I am not at all afraid of the arguments of the Academians, who say, What if you are deceived? For if I am deceived, I am. For he who is not, cannot be deceived..."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite&#95;note-125"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-125">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">There has been debate over how best to characterize James' position. The <i>Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</i> states: "James's commitment to panpsychism remains somewhat controversial, since he also advanced a cogent set of objections against a version of the view, which he labelled the 'mind dust' theory, in chapter six of The Principles of Psychology ([1890] 1981). These objections are the inspiration for the so-called 'combination problem', around which much of the twenty first century literature on panpsychism focuses."</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite&#95;note-Metzinger-72"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Metzinger_72-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333433106" /><cite id="CITEREFHarris" class="citation web cs1"><a href="/wiki/Sam_Harris" title="Sam Harris">Harris, Sam</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://samharris.org/subscriber-extras/96-nature-consciousness/">"Making Sense #96"</a>. <i>SamHarris.org</i>. Sam Harris<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">27 August</span> 2020</span>. <q>(25.45) TM:I think it will not be a mystery. Life is not a mystery anymore, but a hundred and fifty years ago many people thought that this is an irreducible mystery. (25:57) Harris:So you're not a fan anymore, if you ever were, of the framing by David Chalmers of the Hard Problem of Consciousness? Metzinger: No, that's so boring. I mean, that's last century. I mean, you know, we all respect Dave [Chalmers], and we know he is very smart and has got a very fast mind, no debate about that. But conceivability arguments are just very, very weak. If you have an ill-defined folk psychological umbrella term like "consciousness", then you can pull off all kinds of scenarios and zombie thought experiments. It doesn't really… It helped to clarify some issues in the mid 90's, but the consciousness community has listened to this and just moved on. I mean nobody of the serious researchers in the field thinks about this anymore, but it has taken on like a folkloristic life of its own. A lot of people talk about the Hard Problem who wouldn't be able to state what it consists in now.</q></cite><span title="ctx&#95;ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft&#95;val&#95;fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=SamHarris.org&amp;rft.atitle=Making+Sense+%2396&amp;rft.aulast=Harris&amp;rft.aufirst=Sam&amp;rft&#95;id=https%3A%2F%2Fsamharris.org%2Fsubscriber-extras%2F96-nature-consciousness%2F&amp;rfr&#95;id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHard+problem+of+consciousness" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
